<h1 id="bookTitle" class="bookTitle" itemprop="name">
      Lolita
</h1>

<div id="bookAuthors" class="stacked">
  <span class='by smallText'>by</span>
<span itemprop='author' itemscope='' itemtype='http://schema.org/Person'>

  <span id="freeText11102163897976874083" style="display:none">Humbert Humbert - scholar, aesthete and romantic - has fallen completely and utterly in love with Lolita Haze, his landlady's gum-snapping, silky skinned twelve-year-old daughter. Reluctantly agreeing to marry Mrs Haze just to be close to Lolita, Humbert suffers greatly in the pursuit of romance; but when Lo herself starts looking for attention elsewhere, he will carry her off on a desperate cross-country misadventure, all in the name of Love. Hilarious, flamboyant, heart-breaking and full of ingenious word play, Lolita is an immaculate, unforgettable masterpiece of obsession, delusion and lust.</span>

<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Very. In the first 100 pages I had to take at least 3 breaks to restrain myself from breaking something. I guess I can look at the sinful emotions and<a href="#" class="jsShow showAction">â¦more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Very. In the first 100 pages I had to take at least 3 breaks to restrain myself from breaking something. I guess I can look at the sinful emotions and desires the same way any taboo love, but this psycho has a way of talking about his dark corners like a bad religion and that creeps me the hell out.<a href="#" class="jsHide hideAction">(less)</a></span></span>

<div id="review_58706032" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/58706032" />
  <a href="/user/show/2250020-tatiana" class="left imgcol" title="Tatiana"><img alt="Tatiana" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/users/1249783659p2/2250020.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/58706032?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Jan 04, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2250020-tatiana" class="user" title="Tatiana">Tatiana</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/11013199-lolita" class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9780739345672">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">those who will not fall for Humbert's lies</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=1001" class="actionLinkLite">1001</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=2012" class="actionLinkLite">2012</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=national-book-award" class="actionLinkLite">national-book-award</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer58706032" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14004758840883832615">I wasn't even going to write a review of <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7604.Lolita" title="Lolita by Vladimir Nabokov">Lolita</a> after finishing it, because, honestly, how many reviews does this classic need? That is, until I started pocking around and reading what others have to say about it. Many reactions to this book are puzzling to me. In this world of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jerry_Sandusky" rel="nofollow">Jerry Sanduskys</a> and such, there are still people who find this "erotic," who in the end feel compassionate towards the narrator, who think that Lolita was the one who seduced and manipulated poor Humbert? Well, I beg to d</span>
  <span id="freeText14004758840883832615" style="display:none">I wasn't even going to write a review of <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7604.Lolita" title="Lolita by Vladimir Nabokov">Lolita</a> after finishing it, because, honestly, how many reviews does this classic need? That is, until I started pocking around and reading what others have to say about it. Many reactions to this book are puzzling to me. In this world of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jerry_Sandusky" rel="nofollow">Jerry Sanduskys</a> and such, there are still people who find this "erotic," who in the end feel compassionate towards the narrator, who think that Lolita was the one who seduced and manipulated poor Humbert? Well, I beg to differ. <br><br><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7604.Lolita" title="Lolita by Vladimir Nabokov">Lolita</a> is as erotic as <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/439288.Speak" title="Speak by Laurie Halse Anderson">Speak</a> is <a href="http://madwomanintheforest.com/this-guy-thinks-speak-is-pornography/" rel="nofollow">pornographic</a>. As for favorable opinions of Humbert, I guess it is possible this effect can be attributed to Nabokov's mastery of deception. Clearly, Humbert still, half a century after the novel's publication, manages to fool readers, and himself, into believing that he is a dedicated, caring lover, wounded and changed by an early tragic romance. Only occasionally does the truth bleed through his self-delusion - Lolita's wistful glance at a child sitting on his father's lap, a simple act that is forever sallied by Humbert's filth, her disinterest in life, her resignation to satisfy him for pocket money and permission to participate in a school play. No, Humbert did not fool me into feeling sorry for him. <br><br>On a technical level, <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7604.Lolita" title="Lolita by Vladimir Nabokov">Lolita</a> deserves full 5 stars - the language, the wit, the world play! - I don't think I've ever read anything like this before. But emotionally this look into a pedophile's psyche is so disgusting, I can't quite bring myself to rate it so. Humbert is so sickly real to me, with his apologies, justifications of his behavior, cowardice, sob stories and bending of reality, how does an author create someone like this? How did Nabokov get such an intimate knowledge of someone so despicable? </span>
  <a data-text-id="14004758840883832615" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/5022264-ian-heidin-seek" class="user" title="Ian Heidin-Seek">Ian Heidin-Seek</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/70989.The_Annotated_Lolita" class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141185040">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer150036434" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4470671521356111395"><strong>Between the Covers</strong><br><br>This afternoon, having just re-read "Lolita", I asked my local bookseller (a lovely lady in her late 50's) if she had ever read it.<br>She replied firmly, âNoâ¦and Iâm not going to either. Heâs a paedophile.â<br>A bit taken aback, I enquired further, âWho? The author or the character?â<br>Fortunately, she replied, âThe character.â<br>For me, this exchange showed how much âLolitaâ can still sharply divide opinion, even within lovers of fiction.<br>This wasnât the conversation I had been hoping for.<br>I</span>
  <span id="freeText4470671521356111395" style="display:none"><strong>Between the Covers</strong><br><br>This afternoon, having just re-read "Lolita", I asked my local bookseller (a lovely lady in her late 50's) if she had ever read it.<br>She replied firmly, âNoâ¦and Iâm not going to either. Heâs a paedophile.â<br>A bit taken aback, I enquired further, âWho? The author or the character?â<br>Fortunately, she replied, âThe character.â<br>For me, this exchange showed how much âLolitaâ can still sharply divide opinion, even within lovers of fiction.<br>This wasnât the conversation I had been hoping for.<br>I had read âLolitaâ in a couple of days, less time than my work commitments normally allow me, but I found it incredibly easy to read.<br>Even though I was taking notes, even though I was conscious that Nabokov was playing games (even if I didnât always know what game), even though there were unfamiliar words I should have looked up, I was constantly drawn towards the conclusion.<br>I wanted to talk to someone about my experience straight away.<br>My cheeks were still flushed, my nerve endings were still tingling, I had experienced the âspine thrill of delightâ, I felt like I had just had sex with a book.<br>Now, not being a smoker, all I needed was some post-coital conversation.<br>And there was no one around to converse with.<br>And the book wasnât giving away any more of its secrets than it already had.<br>Nor was it going to tell me I had been a Good Reader or that it had appreciated my attentiveness.<br>It was back between the covers, challenging me to start again.<br><br><strong>Three Act Word Play</strong><br><br>At a superficial level, âLolitaâ is a relatively straight-forward novel.<br>Once you know that it concerns sexual relations between 37 year old Humbert Humbert and 12 year old Dolores âLolitaâ Hayes, you just about know the plot.<br>Thereâs a beginning, a middle and an end.<br>A grooming, a consummation, an aftermath.<br>Nabokov makes of his material a three act play.<br>And he does so playfully, seductively, lyrically, charmingly, amusingly, dangerously.<br>To this day, I cannot look at Humbertâs initials âH.H.â without pronouncing them in German, âHa Haâ, and wondering whether the joke is on us.<br>Beneath the skin of the novel, there is much more.<br>There is a whole complex living organism.<br>You can lose yourself within its arms for days, weeks, months, a lifetime.<br>As long as your love of wordplay, your love of words and play, will permit you.<br>Again, at a superficial level, there is an almighty conflict between morality and aesthetics happening between the pages.<br>Whether or not Nabokov deliberately put the conflict there, he put the subject matter there.<br>We, the readers, can supply our own conflict in the way we read his novel.<br>Nabokov knew the subject matter would inflame us, if not our desires, then at least our morals, our sense of righteousness.<br>Morality and aesthetics are intertwined within the fabric of the novel.<br>They embrace each other in one long death roll, just like Humbert Humbert and Clare Quilty.<br>We watch their interaction, open-mouthed, open-minded, but ultimately they have to be pulled apart or separated.<br>When they are together, they are one.<br>When they are apart, they are each otherâs double.<br><br><strong>The Morality of the Story</strong><br><br>There is no doubt that sexual relations between an adult and a minor are not just immoral, but criminal as well.<br>That is an unquestionable fact.<br>From a legal point of view, the motive of the adult is irrelevant to the proof of the crime.<br>The consent of the minor is irrelevant to the proof of the crime.<br>If Humbert had been charged with an offence of sexual relations with a minor, he would have had no legal defence.<br>Any question as to whether Lolita really seduced Humbert would have been irrelevant.<br>In fact, the evidence might not even have been admissible, except potentially as part of the determination of the penalty.<br>In other words, even if it was relevant to penalty, it was not relevant to guilt.<br>Because morality is a social construct that depends on collective endorsement, he had no moral defence either.<br>The personal views of the individual are not really that relevant to societyâs determination that an act is immoral.<br>The choice of the individual is to comply or offend.<br><br><strong>Of Traps and Cages</strong><br><br>Humbert offended not just once, but untold numerous times over two years.<br>He carefully planned his seduction, he set his trap, he caught his prey, even if someone might want to argue that this 12 year old seductress walked voluntarily into the trap.<br>Having freed Lolita from the trap, he imprisoned her in a cage, and repeated his crime.<br>Again, someone could argue that she had plenty of opportunities to flee the cage (which she eventually did).<br>But Humbert surrounded Lolita with an elaborate system of self-doubt that convinced her that she would become a ward of the state if they were found out.<br><br><strong>The Legality of the Confession</strong><br><br>âLolitaâ is written from Humbertâs point of view.<br>It is not just a recollection in his mind, it is a formal, written document.<br>He sat down and wrote it in 56 days between his capture in 1952 (charged only with the crime of murdering Clare Quilty) and his death in prison before his trial could occur.<br>For me, the written document is a fascinating choice of literary device to tell the story.<br>The document becomes a book within a book.<br>While Nabokov obviously wrote it, all that he purports to do is sandwich it between a Foreword and a (much later) Afterword.<br>This device sets up an interesting relationship between Humbert and the reader.<br>For Humbert, it is akin to a confession or a witness statement.<br>To this extent, what he confesses to is clearly enough to convict him of the crime of murder.<br>However, in it, he also sets out details of crimes that, for whatever reason, he was never charged with.<br>If his lawyer had read the document while he was still alive, he would probably have excised all of the other confessions, because they would have prejudiced his clientâs case (at least with respect to penalty).<br><br><strong>The Role of the Jury</strong><br><br>For the reader, the confession defines our relationship to the events that are described.<br>We are cast in the role of a member of the Jury.<br>This device allows heinous moral and criminal acts to be described and read and examined within a legal and therefore legitimate framework.<br>In a sense, the book becomes a report of sorts on legal proceedings.<br>We become legitimate observers and listeners to something that might otherwise have been prurient and offensive and illegal.<br>Yet, we have to do our duty and participate in the legal process, because it is an important part of the justice system.<br>Even though we have a legitimate interest in participating, I wonder whether we are still voyeuristic.<br>Nabokov has trapped us in a game that persuades us that it is serious, but ends up being just as playful and perverse as the subject matter of the crime.<br>In a way, Nabokov makes us complicit in a crime, if not Humbertâs crime, then perhaps our own thought crime.<br>It is also material that, by the time Humbertâs confession is read, both Humbert and Lolita have died of natural causes.<br>Humbert speaks from the other side of death.<br>Nobody is alive, nobody can be hurt any more than they already have.<br><br><strong>The Confessions of an Unreliable Narrator (The Fox and the Peacock)</strong><br><br>I explored these issues, because I wanted to understand Humbertâs motivation for his confession.<br>He is effectively pleading guilty.<br>I donât see any prospect for an insanity defence, even though he seemed to have been in and out of sanatoria at times of crisis.<br>Equally, I donât think that anything he reveals would reduce the penalty for the murder.<br>To do so, he only needed to focus on his concern that Quilty had wronged Lolita in some way even worse than his own actions.<br>But to confess all of these other crimes seems to be counter-productive.<br>Similarly, I donât think he was lying about the detail, I think that he was telling the truth, and that he was telling the truth, so that he could be understood, no more, no less.<br>Humbertâs confession is not just the fiction of a dirty old man, it is not false or fabricated, it is not a mirage.<br>No matter how immoral, no matter how deluded, no matter how selfish and narcissistic, it is his fact, his reality, his truth, his burden, his shame.<br>His actions were the pursuit of a rational man, not an insane one.<br>He was film-star handsome, educated, intellectual, talented, witty, charming, calculating, calculated, dangerous.<br>There is no doubt that he was a talented performer, an exceptional player.<br>However, Humbert is not an actor wearing a mask, performing some other fictional character or version of himself.<br>I believe that we are seeing him for what he really is.<br>He is as cunning, tricky, sly as a fox and as refined, elaborate, attractive as a peacock.<br>His decoration, his ornamentation is part of him, his life, his loins, his sin, his soul.<br>In pursuit of Lolita, he was prepared to lie and deceive in order to achieve his goal.<br>I donât believe that he was prepared to lie to us, if only because there was no point in lying.<br>When occasionally he questions the veracity of his own account, it is solely to question the accuracy of his memory.<br>However, he didnât need to tell lies to achieve leniency, he didnât need to tell the truth for some ulterior motive.<br>By confessing to anything, he would only be found guilty of crimes he hadnât been charged with in addition to the charge of murder he had been accused of.<br>There was no point in confessing to anything extra, other than to tell the truth as he saw it.<br>It wasnât going to get him any sympathy or reduce his penalty, if anything, his disclosures would aggravate his penalty.<br>To this extent, I donât consider Humbert an âunreliable narratorâ.<br>I realise that some might respond that paedophiles are habitual liars and canât help themselves.<br>That might well be the case, but I think it is our horror at his crime, our moral judgment affecting our assessment of the whole of the person and shaping our (aesthetic) response to the book and the character.<br>Perhaps naively, I want to find some good in him.<br>Ultimately, whether or not Humbertâs love was morally wrong, I believe that he wanted us to understand his love and what he learned about his love by the end of his story.<br><br><strong>What We Talk about When We Talk about Humbertâs Love</strong><br><br>Technically, the sexual relations between Humbert and Lolita are not an example of âpaedophiliaâ (which is a sexual preference for a pre-pubescent).<br>While nothing moral or legal turns on the distinction, the sexual relations constitute âhebephiliaâ (which is a sexual preference for a person in the early stage of puberty).<br>The name derives from âHebeâ, the Greek goddess of youth.<br>Her name means youth or prime of life, and she personified both youth and immortality.<br>She was the cup bearer who served nectar to the Olympian Gods to give them everlasting youth.<br><br><strong>First Part (Obsessive Love)</strong><br><br>For me, during the first part of the book, Humbertâs love was forbidden, but genuine.<br>It was a transgressive love, in that it was a love of the particular aesthetic form that youth takes between the ages of ten and fifteen.<br>The body is at its most perfect, it has not started to age, to wrinkle, to fill out, to droop, to deteriorate.<br>After that age, the body starts to age, and he finds that physically unattractive (as in the case of his first wife and Lolita's mother).<br>OK, we all make choices about our love objects.<br>How can we account for our choices?<br>Thereâs no accounting for love.<br>Still, at the heart of this aesthetic approach to love is a fear or disgust at aging and mortality.<br>There is an unreality, a lack of understanding and acceptance of the cycle of life and death, a Peter Pan desire to stay forever young, forever immortal.<br>I also think there is a self-love or narcissism inherent in this aesthetic view.<br>I love the young, because I love the perfect form of my own youth.<br>Since my youth, I have fallen, morally and physically.<br>I therefore have to preserve the visage of my own youth.<br>I wonder whether it is only possible to have this view if you have never had your own biological child.<br>Parenthood is an education in the reality of aging.<br>It is an illusion to believe that you can live and defeat it.<br>But tell that to the cosmetics industry.<br>So far I have talked of love in the abstract.<br>In the first part of the book, I struggled to understand Humbertâs love and the above is what I came up with.<br>I wonât say I had a sympathy for him, but I think I understood him and his love.<br>I even understood his obsessiveness.<br>How many of us, during the first throes of love, trap and oppress our love object, so much so that we are not able to see how oppressive we were, until after the relationship has been consummated, or morphed into something more mature or ended?<br>However, things started to change at the end of the first part (the consummation) and into the second part (the imprisonment).<br>Of course the love had to be consummated, but as unexceptional as the description of the event was, it highlighted the reality that the first part was a trap for Lolita to walk into.<br>As playful and lyrical as the language might have been, it was sinister in intent.<br><br><strong>Second Part (Captivating Love)</strong><br><br>During the second part, having captured Lo, Humbert makes it clear that his love will last no more than three years, to be precise,  1 January, 1947 to 1 January, 1950, which are effectively her 12th to 15th birthdays.<br>After this, statistically at least, Lo will morph out of her nymphet form.<br>So Humbert's love is solely for a definitive phase of her entire life, after which he expects and intends to abandon her.<br>During this phase, Humbertâs goal is to maintain Lolita in captivity, to ensure her availability for him alone. <br>There is no fairy tale promise of âhappily ever afterâ or ââtil death do us partâ in this love action.<br>There is no love or concern for the other, only selfishness and narcissism.<br>I have tried to view the definition of beauty that appeals to Humbert as an aesthetic issue.<br>I have tried to divorce it from morality, so I can understand it better.<br>However, whether I think of it in terms of aesthetics or morality, obsession or love, the fact that it could be switched on and off at such identifiable times turned me against Humbert.<br>He is in control of this feeling called love, at least, he knows with clinical precision when he will return to ânormalityâ or a state of not loving.<br>His love was a drug that he took too knowingly, he knew precisely when the feeling of the drug would wear off.<br>So, I started to believe that there was no loss of self in his love.<br>Instead, it was a heightened or gross act of narcissism.<br>By extension, there was no sense in which he tried to "satisfy" Lo personally or sexually.<br>There was no sense of a mutually satisfying relationship or intercourse (although to be fair, he doesn't go into the sexual detail, except in terms of physical exertion).<br>However, I got the sense that, when it came to consummating his love, it was just about sticking his dick into his love object.<br>OK, lots of sexual relationships can be reduced to this fundamental penetrative act.<br>Some men see femaleness as no more than a receptacle for maleness and its fluid manifestation, the cup into which they spill their seed.<br>However, I started to feel in the second part that Humbert's aim was to defile or despoil the beauty that had appealed to him in the first part (even if it was transgressive).<br>And the three year zone of enchantment highlighted to me that Humbert would just go in search of the next beautiful nymphet to stick his dick into.<br>So it became increasingly apparent to me that he was a serial despoiler of beauty, not a genuine lover or admirer of beauty.<br>There is a hatred or disgust hotwired into this love.<br>You don't normally hate the flowers in your vase when it comes time to remove them and throw them in the dustbin.<br>But you get the sense that Humbert would have been disgusted by his former love objects, his objet d'obsession, the moment that calendar clicked over.<br>Obviously, this same disgust or loss of interest appears in more traditional relationships.<br>It could lie behind the mid-life crisis when the guy runs away with the younger woman.<br>It could explain the inability to accept the inevitability of aging, at least in our partner.<br>It could explain we males who still picture ourselves as the immutable 20 year old who deserves a young and nubile partner (no matter how soft or old or fat or ugly we have become).<br>So Humbertâs love can teach the rest of us something about our own love.<br><br><strong>Last Part (Adult Love Denied)</strong><br><br>I wrote most of my comments about the second part before I had finished reading the last part of the novel.<br>I have to emphasise that most of what turned me against Humbert came from my reaction to his own words.<br>Neither he nor Nabokov held back the material that would make me hate him.<br>Still, I read on, firmly in their constrictive embrace, until chapter 29, when Humbert and the seventeen year old, married and pregnant Dolores meet again.<br>What you think of Humbert and his love, whether or not you think he is lying, depends on your interpretation of the confessions in this chapter:<br><br><em>ââ¦there she was with her ruined looks and her adult, rope-veined narrow hands and her gooseflesh white arms, and her shallow ears, and her unkempt armpits, there she was (my Lolita!), hopelessly worn at seventeen, with that babyâ¦ and I looked and I looked at her, and knew as clearly as I know I am to die, that I loved her more than anything I had ever seen or imagined on earth, or hoped for anywhere elseâ¦<br>âWhat I used to pamper among the tangled vines of my heartâ¦had dwindled to its essence: sterile and selfish vice, all that I cancelled and cursedâ¦<br>âYou may jeer at me, and threaten to clear the court, but until I am gagged and half-throttled, I will shout my poor truth.<br>âI insist the world know how much I loved my Lolita, this Lolita, pale and polluted, and big with anotherâs child, but still gray-eyed, still sooty-lashed, still auburn and almond, still Carmencita, still mine.â</em><br><br>This is just one part of Humbertâs journey.<br>He realised that he still loved her outside the hebephile zone.<br>However, he still clung to âhisâ Lolita, the Lolita of his deluded version of love.<br>Obviously, Dolores is and never was âhisâ version of reality, she was her own person, and she declines his love a second time.<br>Only then does he recognise that he âdid not know a thing about [his] darlingâs mindâ or that âa North American girl-child named Dolores Haze had been deprived of her childhood by a maniacâ.<br>Then he quotes âan old poetâ (presumably Nabokov himself):<br><br><em>âThe moral sense in mortals is the duty<br>âWe have to pay on mortal sense of beauty.â</em><br><br>In other words, you canât just indulge an aesthetic  sense of beauty at the expense of a real human being, it comes attached to and constrained by morality.<br>Morality, taboo and the law work together to protect innocence and beauty from those who would defile and despoil it.<br>He was not above the law, he was no Nietzschian Superman.<br>He was the fool in his own play.<br><br><strong>The Tragedy</strong><br><br>There are suggestions that Nabokov saw Humbertâs story as a tragedy, that Humbert only realised that he genuinely loved Dolores by conventional standards when it was too late.<br>That might be so, but Humbert only had himself to blame.<br>He was a victim of his own hand, and his tragedy was nothing compared with the one he made Dolores endure, so that he, too selfishly for love, could have his âLolitaâ.</span>

<div id="review_133726933" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/133726933" />
  <a href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may" class="left imgcol" title="Emily May"><img alt="Emily May" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/users/1402215455p2/4622890.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/133726933?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">May 06, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may" class="user" title="Emily May">Emily May</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=fave-classics" class="actionLinkLite">fave-classics</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=book-your-brains-out" class="actionLinkLite">book-your-brains-out</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer133726933" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1612706591975104716">Now, this is going to be embarrassing to admit.<br><br>As we all should know, reading and enjoying a book is largely about interpretation. People are not the same and we all view things differently, one individual might see a relationship in a book as "passionate" while another could see it as "damaging". When characters make bad decisions, some will view it as stupidity and others will view it as an accurate representation of humanity's imperfections. Not only that, but time often changes the way one p</span>
  <span id="freeText1612706591975104716" style="display:none">Now, this is going to be embarrassing to admit.<br><br>As we all should know, reading and enjoying a book is largely about interpretation. People are not the same and we all view things differently, one individual might see a relationship in a book as "passionate" while another could see it as "damaging". When characters make bad decisions, some will view it as stupidity and others will view it as an accurate representation of humanity's imperfections. Not only that, but time often changes the way one person sees things. A teenager does not usually have the same outlook on life and relationships that someone of thirty does, and neither of them have the same outlook as someone of seventy does.<br><br>So it's time that I admit, when reading this at thirteen, my younger brain actually romanticised Humbert's depravity and saw the relationship between him and Lolita as some tragic love affair that could never work out for the obvious reasons. It was (surprise, surprise) <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/58706032">Tatiana's review</a> that made me wonder if I'd had a screw loose when reading this years ago, her interpretation was so far from what I remembered that I simply had to find time for a re-read. This summer, I did just that. I am going to point my shameful finger of blame at my age when I first read it, I was as fooled by Humbert as the young Lolita was.<br><br>Humbert is not a reliable narrator, his declaration that Lolita was responsible for seducing him is repulsive and wrong. Because, in the end, an adult has no excuse for having sex with a child, even if they're walking around half-naked and offering it up - adults have a responsibility not to take advantage of children, and I now realise how this case is no exception. This is not some tragic romantic tale about forbidden love, it is the story of how a grown man repeatedly raped a young girl. The fact that it is so easy to be taken in by him either says something about how brilliant a writer Nabokov is (which he is), or how much society still loves to blame the victim.<br><br>I don't know whether to feel better about my original feelings or be horrified that even the description for the <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11013199-lolita">audiobook</a> describes the novel as: <em>"a love story almost shocking in its beauty and tenderness."</em> And I also know that I have no right to criticise other people who saw it in such a way, but I would ask you to read it again, to look beyond Humbert's snivelling and self-pity, to see the man who considers murdering a woman so he can be free to have sex with her twelve year old daughter, the man who feels sorry for himself when a pubescent girl doesn't want to have sex with him because she's still hurt from the last time. Is that love? Maybe it was for a thirteen year old looking through Humbert's perverted eyes, but I'm glad I understand it better now.<br><br>Nabokov has written a brilliant and disturbing novel, my opinion of it hasn't changed in that respect. I found it surprisingly easy to read and became absorbed quickly - even all those years ago. His portrayal of Humbert's perverted mind is scarily good, perhaps even too good if people can so easily be convinced to side with a paedophile - which is often regarded as the ultimate crime of all, isn't it? Even cold-blooded murderers go after prisoners who've messed with kids. And, as much as I feel ashamed for being so taken in by Humbert, I know that it's not just me who was fooled. Hell, even the GR description proves it. But, believe me, Lolita is a victim and no amount of saddening flashbacks to Humbert's past can change that. <br><br><a href="http://thebookgeek.co.uk" rel="nofollow">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.leafmarks.com/emilymay" rel="nofollow">Leafmarks</a> | <a href="https://www.facebook.com/thebookgeekreviews" rel="nofollow">Facebook</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/thebookgeek01" rel="nofollow">Twitter</a> | <a href="http://instagram.com/emleemay" rel="nofollow">Instagram</a> | <a href="http://mycountryheart.co.uk/" rel="nofollow">Tumblr</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="1612706591975104716" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_178072" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/178072" />
  <a href="/user/show/18657-rolls" class="left imgcol" title="Rolls"><img alt="Rolls" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/users/1392343453p2/18657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/178072?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Mar 13, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/18657-rolls" class="user" title="Rolls">Rolls</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_3" title="liked it">3 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/18133.Lolita" class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679723165">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">pervs</span></div>

        

    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer178072" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13364967216685246890">An old friend used to say that "Ulysses" was a good book to read but not a good book to "read". After reading "Lolita" I understand what he meant.<br><br>Nabokov was a man obsessed with word games and this book is crammed cover to cover with many brilliant examples. Language delighted the man and that certainly comes across. What makes this acheivement even more amazing was that English was his third or fourth language. It is mind blowing that he or anyone could write so fluidly in a "foreign" tongue. I</span>
  <span id="freeText13364967216685246890" style="display:none">An old friend used to say that "Ulysses" was a good book to read but not a good book to "read". After reading "Lolita" I understand what he meant.<br><br>Nabokov was a man obsessed with word games and this book is crammed cover to cover with many brilliant examples. Language delighted the man and that certainly comes across. What makes this acheivement even more amazing was that English was his third or fourth language. It is mind blowing that he or anyone could write so fluidly in a "foreign" tongue. If this was enough to make a novel great then this would be one of my top ten.<br><br>But what if, as a reader, you demand that an author make his characters compelling and the narrative involving? I would say then that this book is not for you. Humbert and Dolores Haze (Lolita) only ever (to my mind) become three dimensional at odd moments here and there. He comes off as a mincing, foppish but ultimately unbelievable sort. I never bought into him until very near the end when for a few sentences Nabokov makes his remorse credible. But it is too late for that. I was already annoyed as hell by his rococo narration. The character of Lolita as well is shrill and one note through out. Only intermittently does she come across as worthy of compassion. <br><br>As for the story, once the seduction takes place it loses a lot of its forward momentum. It begins to feel repetitive and only comes alive again when Humbert reaches the very end of his self control and attempts to lash out at one he believes wronged him. All in all I think this is a book that could stand to lose about fifty pages. <br><br>There is much to love about it though. It could have been truly replusive. Nabokov knew that his concept was already off putting and that the execution need not be so. Rather than serving up spewing fluids and hungry orifices he treats us to healthy doses of wit and charm. Bravo!<br><br>"Lolita" is obviously literature with a capital "L." It is a work by a man of letters who happened to be a genius - for that reason alone it deserves reading. Just don't be surprised that once you're done you don't feel like recommending it to anyone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13364967216685246890" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_114735639" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/114735639" />
  <a href="/user/show/2447803-jason" class="left imgcol" title="Jason"><img alt="Jason" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/users/1246308632p2/2447803.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/114735639?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Aug 16, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2447803-jason" class="user" title="Jason">Jason</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/218878.Lolita" class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780613706254">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer114735639" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10486962192435839387">Nymph.  Nymphet.  Nymphetiquette.  Nymphology.  Nymphism.  I will never think of 12 year old girls the same way.  Thereâs a stain on my brain.  The power of this book is that itâs creepy and taboo, but the pedophilia and incest is so damn plausible.  Thereâs a criminal, upsetting proclivity of the subject matter, but the whole thing is oiled with reason--SAY IT AINT SO.  Itâs deviant, queer, puerile, and yet ever so human, darkly human, perverted in the corner.<br><br><em>Lolita</em> lingers in my mind, like an accidenta</span>
  <span id="freeText10486962192435839387" style="display:none">Nymph.  Nymphet.  Nymphetiquette.  Nymphology.  Nymphism.  I will never think of 12 year old girls the same way.  Thereâs a stain on my brain.  The power of this book is that itâs creepy and taboo, but the pedophilia and incest is so damn plausible.  Thereâs a criminal, upsetting proclivity of the subject matter, but the whole thing is oiled with reason--SAY IT AINT SO.  Itâs deviant, queer, puerile, and yet ever so human, darkly human, perverted in the corner.<br><br><em>Lolita</em> lingers in my mind, like an accidental glance at the mid-day sun.  I believe this book will have a permanent effect on me.  Iâm thankful, but cautious.  Itâs a book that I experienced, not so much as read.  There are 2 components to this book that radically affected me, the writing and the subject matter.<br><br><u>The Writing</u><br>I have never read another book written quite like <em>Lolita</em>.  The writing has depth, layer upon layer, strata against strata, texture among texture.  Itâs a palimpsest of clues and anagrams and reference.  The author has absolute command of the English and French and Latin language.  And yet, among the $4 dollar words and bourgeoisie lit crit, Nabakov plays with the language.  He invents words.  He hyphenates them.  He nymphorizes them.  Itâs a gamboling and frolicking story in the rarefied air of an unrestrained, unapologetic and unadulterated polyhistoric writer.  Itâs subtle and raw at the same time; itâs pure.  Pure, like what happens in your neighborhood behind closed doors, just before an arrest.  He incorporates a dry, brittle sense of humor--even a bit of sass.  He taunts the reader to follow.  He dares the reader to like and enjoy Humbert Humbert.  He pokes you in the eye.  He scandalizes you, but with a pen that is at once brutal and sensitive, but always careful.  There are echoes of Joyce and Poe.<br><br>The story is a retrospective from...from...from where?  What?  Prison.  Ostensibly.  And yet, there hasnât been a trial yet--no judgement.  Nabakov tantalizes you, âladies and gentlemen of the jury,â to pass judgement on Humbert Humbert yourself.  Are you willing?  Or will you just turn your head, wincing?<br><br>The writing is breathless, eloquent, exacting, alluring, inventive, sexy, pleading, conceited, lurid, savory, languid, and slyly self-deprecating.  The author is flagrant, unapologetic, a dandy even.  He whiffles the writing in so many little stylistic flourishes.  He writes sentences and paragraphs in ways that I would never have guessed to try.  Itâs insanely periodic writing; I grab my head akimbo in pure awe of the sentences.  I peeked at an annotated version for 20 pages at a local big-box book store.  Wow, thereâs so many levels to this writing, of so much I was ignorant.  Did you know that under the shocking story of pedophilia, Nabakov is carrying on a paper chase with clues on almost every page?  Yes, thereâs a whole other plane of conversation hidden below the written words--grammatically, semantically, nymphatically.  Theyâre buried in the french words, the double entendres, the onomatopoeia, the puns, the metonymy, the symbols, the rhyming, the nymphventions.  Palimpsest âladies and gentlemen.â<br><br><u>The Subject Matter</u><br>We all know <em>Lolita</em> is supposed to be shocking, revolting even, many people not able to finish it.  Titillating, serious fiction about pedophilia is the clear edge of the literary envelope, something banned in many different communities, even today.  At this particular time in our democracy, as one of the freest countries in the world, and the most progressive, we champion human rights and place a huge penalty on crimes against minors.  In this spirit, we are supposed to decry and detest the subject matter in this book, and lambast the author.  People are arrested and put on community rosters for crimes against minors.  This 300+ page book chronicles a crime against a minor.  Nabakov makes this an even more difficult sexual arrangement for his readers to contemplate, because the 12 year old is an eager, compliant and willing partner to the crime.<br><br>In <em>Lolita</em> the protagonist is a criminal and his actions unforgivable.  BUT, if there was any method to his madness, it would have to be this:<br><br>Humans share a cephalization process in common with most vertebrates.  We developed cerebral hemispheres several million years ago (progressing beyond our closest ancestors), and more recently than that, humans learned how to use the cerebral cortex to reason, judge, cognate, and intuit.  But, hundreds of millions of years ago, way down the taxonomic branch, we shared with other vertebrates a common mesencephalon and rhombencephalon, the midbrain and hindbrain.  Tucked up under our marvelous, modern cortex, the midbrain and hindbrain, called the brain stem, are comprised of the pons, cerebellum, and yes, the MEDULLA OBLONGATA!  These are ancient, compact organs.  They are the most âanimalâ part of our brains.  They are in control of the lower order mental functions, the basic mechanistic functions upon which everything else depends.  You can lose part of your cortex and still function as a human.  You cannot, however, lose any of your brain stem without losing basic animal function.  The brain stem is innately integral to life.<br><br>Itâs from this midbrain we get reflex, instinct, coordinated movement, sex drive, fight-or-flight, and a whole range of metabolic regulation for all organs in the rest of the body.  The impulses (the input, the direction, the priority) originating in these Mesozoic Era brain organs are powerful.  The cerebral cortex would be remiss to block an impulse from this deep, ancient brain--even if it could stop the impulse in time.  Itâs difficult for our human cortex to constrain an electrical input from the animal brain stem.  What comes from the stem is automatically life-sustaining, life-preserving, and high priority.  The cortex usually plays catch-up to brain stem messaging.<br><br>But humans do it all the time.  Itâs called reason, judgement, cognition, and conscience.  Itâs called being civilized.   Itâs keeping in check our vertebrate impulses.<br><br>Enter Humbert Humbert.  He suffers an atavistic urge to procreate with young nymphets.  This is a social problem driven and turbocharged by the midbrain.  He understands (his cortex understands) that the culture of the late 1940s and early 1950s find this taboo and perverse, definitely criminal.  But our poor Humberto doesnât care.  He reasons with his midbrain, and pleads to us, "the jury."  In the not too distant past within our own Western culture, and certainly in modern cultures of tribal peoples, 12 year old girls are ready to mate.  Lolita has already menstruated and had sex with a boy her age.  In many cultures of the world, Lolita would be given up as a wife in exchange for dowries of cattle, land, political favor.  The whole story, then, brings this American taboo to a moral question.  And its a question that you--modern citizen--find uncomfortable, like I do.<br><br>Even more disturbing, Nabakov makes Humby Humberty a caring, loving, protective paternal figure that wishes Lolita the best in life.  There is no direct, lewd reference to the act of sex; nothing salacious; nothing pornographic.  No, that would be too easy to damn Humbo to the devil.  Instead, Nabakov explores the possibility that real love may exist betwain the tween.<br><br>Iâm not too happy to report a phenomenon that happens to men of sexual capacity, always and forever.  Itâs an impulse from the midbrain, and it pushes through all that civilization-ing.  Itâs happened to all men (I know because itâs been a topic of conversation in many different social settings to which I was eye-witness).  Take for example a young woman of 16 or 17 years.  From afar I see a body in bikini, I see a tight, athletic form, I see a bronzed body wearing clothes much too revealing, and immediately the midbrain excites the male sex drive.  Upon closer approach, Iâm horrified to see that this nubile figure is much too young for me.  Am I perverted?  Criminal thoughts?  I donât think so.  The midbrain wants to ensure successful mating, and for hundreds of millions of years, sexual mating, to be maximally effective, and to outlast environmental exigencies, was driven down to the earliest age that could conceive offspring.  So that dastardly urge men experience around cheerleaders, or girls at the beach that look as healthy and trim as fresh gazelles--itâs not right dammit, and most of us keep it in check, but there it is and itâs nagging, and I wish it away.  But no, I think it will remain and haunt me at times like it haunts all men--your men--your brothers and your fathers and your lovers.  I look away in disgust of myself, call myself a âdirty old man,â whatever it takes to recalibrate my thoughts.  It happens occasionally--that oogling--but I keep it in check.  But if you think society has civilized itself away from this midbrain urge, type into google the words: âlist of sexual predators in my area.â  You will see a Mesozoic characteristic come alive. <em>{note to self, this paragraph may need to be reworded...a very good chance most people will misconstrue it...as if I was pardoning the midbrain urge...or worse, that I pardon Humbert Humbert...not the case at all}</em><br><br>So thatâs why at the beginning I said this story was so damn plausible and upsetting and âoiled with reason,â and darkly human.  Pedophilia and incest has occurred, is occurring, and will always occur.  That beast of a midbrain!<br><br>A very important read for 20th century literature.<br><br>New words: incondite, contretemps, swain, alembic, tombal, purblind, dulcet, treacle, edusively, viatic, selenian</span>
  <a data-text-id="10486962192435839387" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_9795517" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9795517" />
  <a href="/user/show/645835-n" class="left imgcol" title="N"><img alt="N" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/9795517?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Dec 01, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/645835-n" class="user" title="N">N</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_3" title="liked it">3 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/18133.Lolita" class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679723165">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/645835-n?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/645835-n?shelf=not-on-kindle" class="actionLinkLite">not-on-kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9795517" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6272360768877493197">This book was disappointing and over-hyped.<br><br>When people talk about this book, they say things like it will "change the way you think" or that it's disquieting because it makes the reader sympathize with a pedophile.  I thought wow, that much be worth reading.<br><br>Now I wonder if I read the same book as everyone else, or if *that* many people have misinterpreted it.  It started out great:  Humbert Humbert, the narrator, discusses different societies in the past that found it acceptable for very young wome</span>
  <span id="freeText6272360768877493197" style="display:none">This book was disappointing and over-hyped.<br><br>When people talk about this book, they say things like it will "change the way you think" or that it's disquieting because it makes the reader sympathize with a pedophile.  I thought wow, that much be worth reading.<br><br>Now I wonder if I read the same book as everyone else, or if *that* many people have misinterpreted it.  It started out great:  Humbert Humbert, the narrator, discusses different societies in the past that found it acceptable for very young women to have sex with much older men.  He also thinks his pedophilia might be the result of his desire to regain something of a romance he had when he was a teenager, wherein the girl he was in love with died.  I sympathized with him here and we were moving into morally grey territory, so I thought the book was on track to be everything everyone says it is.  If he and a young girl fell in love with each other, I would certainly question a lot of things I thought before.<br><br>Nope.  Nothing between him and Lolita can be described as love.  Lets see, he detested Lolita's personality. He wanted to impregnate Lolita so he could molest her children and their grandchildren when she was too old for him to find attractive. He would rape her despite the fact that she cried every time. He would rape her when she was seriously ill. He mind-fucked her thoroughly. He physically abused her.  He manipulated her into sex acts by paying her money and other things she wanted, effectively turning her into a prostitute.<br><br>Needless to say, I felt no moral ambiguity when the novel ended. He was just a crazy pervert. That doesn't make me rethink what love is at all, since love entered nowhere in the equation -- it was the lust of a psychologically unstable man. I wasn't disquieted by any sort of sympathy for him; past the first couple chapters it becomes clear that he's just a horrible person who tries to justify his pedophilia with things that don't sound so bad.<br><br>In fact, the novel just reaffirmed what I thought before, that pedophiles are bad people who ruin children's lives, and on the whole it seemed completely pointless.<br><br>What's worse is that it's a fairly slow read, with fifty pages passing at a time with nothing happening.  Nabokov's prose is impressive and clever, but about two-thirds of the writing served no purpose so much of that same prose actually diluted and detracted from the book.  I would say to myself, "That's an amazing description of something completely pointless."  He seemed to just like to hear himself talking at points, and his afterward seems to confirm this.  I feel like the book would have been better off if those passages had been omitted.<br><br>However, I can appreciate the realism of Humbert Humbert's character.  In fact, the one thing the story had going for it was realism.  It just felt pointless by the time I finished, when I was expecting something much more groundbreaking.  It might be fair to blame all the people who over-hyped the book, rather than blame Nabokov.  Removed from the hype its merits are easier to see.  <br><br>The more I think about it, perhaps many others *have* misinterpreted the book.  It seemed to me that Nabokov's purpose was to punish the narrator.  I can see the appeal of that, though I still feel it was pointless.  And it's certainly less lofty than some of the things the other interpretations say, but at least it's more accurate.  <br><br>To be honest, I'm a little irritated that I went into reading the book based on what appears to be a completely off-base interpretation, because I can't quite undo that in my mind.  On the one hand it seems unfair to criticize an author on the basis that they didn't change how I think, but I'm not sure how I could have avoided it, since the cover of my edition has a quote that calls it, "the only convincing love story of our time."<br><br>It's not a horrible book, but it's probably the most overrated book I've ever read.<br></span>
  <a data-text-id="6272360768877493197" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3004515" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3004515" />
  <a href="/user/show/188169-namrirru" class="left imgcol" title="Namrirru"><img alt="Namrirru" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/users/1184278439p2/188169.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/3004515?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Dec 04, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/188169-namrirru" class="user" title="Namrirru">Namrirru</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/7606.The_Annotated_Lolita" class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679727293">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/188169-namrirru?shelf=russian" class="actionLinkLite">russian</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/188169-namrirru?shelf=e" class="actionLinkLite">e</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3004515" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6051591268988146538">Nabokov often writes his novels in the perspective of detestable villains. You never like them, you're never supposed to like them, and Nabokov doesn't like them either. He slaps them around and humiliates them. And in the end, they pay the price for their sins. Readers never seem to realize this. They become immersed in the psychology of the book and feel defiled by it all. Instead, they should sit back and watch the bastards suffer. The stories are written in their own view so that makes the p</span>
  <span id="freeText6051591268988146538" style="display:none">Nabokov often writes his novels in the perspective of detestable villains. You never like them, you're never supposed to like them, and Nabokov doesn't like them either. He slaps them around and humiliates them. And in the end, they pay the price for their sins. Readers never seem to realize this. They become immersed in the psychology of the book and feel defiled by it all. Instead, they should sit back and watch the bastards suffer. The stories are written in their own view so that makes the punishment all the more sweet. The reader knows exactly what the scheisemeister is feeling - pain, pain, pain. That's one of the reasons I like Nabokov so much. The bad guys really get it. It's not just getting killed or caught at the end, you really feel their anguish. Mmm... schadenfreude." <br><br>I sort of believe the reason why most of the characters are 2 dimensional is precisely because of who is potraying them, a depraved person. A depraved person who commits terrible, unforgivable crimes against people. How could he commit those crimes if he saw them as the human beings that they are? It's easier for a crook to swindle if he dehumanizes his victims. At the end of Lolita, Lolita transfigures. She's a sensitive, care-worn woman, but only because HH realizes her as such. That's why he can murder the man who betrayed her at the end. He was a filthy mongrel that deserved to die for what he did to her. "She groped for words. I supplied them mentally ("He broke my heart. You merely broke my life")." I don't remember, but this could be the first time he "supplied [words] mentally" in a way that's true and unselfish. He finally understood her as a person and sacrificed himself to revenge her. Perhaps, HH's only redeeming quality. <br><br>In Nabokov's books where the villain is the protagonist, the only other charachter with real depth or psychology is a character who the protagonists loves. The little daughter in Laughter in the Dark, Lolita at the end the novel, Despair? The protagonist in that one is a sociopath and doesn't give his novel-mates anything, but their personalities pop out. You can feel them from the distance. Other novels I've read by him don't exactly fit this mold. In Pnin, everyone is a little characterized but still quite real, Pale Fire is written by an almost-villain and you love everyone but him, especially the wife of the poet, Invitation to a Beheading, not even the main protagonist was very real. He had the same consciousness and feelings that a "K" would have in one of Kafka's novels. But he had no believable history, it's all just a dreamscape that doesn't have half the terror as Kafka's novels have. I never finished Ada or Ador. It's Lolita x 10 in smuttiness. "Is this really necessary??" And it wasn't believable either. Just a fantasy. <br><br>Of course, not all his novels are going to follow the same formula, but they are written by the same writer, the same mind. So I'm still working on him. I really think he's one of the best writers of the 20th century. He doesn't just tell a story, he explores the psyche and human perceptions, how a certain person feels, sees, or reacts to things. If they were normal people, it wouldn't be interesting, but he picks villains or eccentrics. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6051591268988146538" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_19247366" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/19247366" />
  <a href="/user/show/733629-chris" class="left imgcol" title="Chris"><img alt="Chris" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/users/1199331851p2/733629.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/19247366?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">May 04, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/733629-chris" class="user" title="Chris">Chris</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_2" title="it was ok">2 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/18133.Lolita" class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679723165">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">any literate fans of Casey Parker</span></div>

        

    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer19247366" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4110702374742712810">*Ranked as one of the Top 100 Fiction of the 20th Century*<br>Iâm not quite sure how to put this in words.  Hell, Iâm not sure what I intend to say, so this is going to be ugly.  If you want to sit in on this exercise be my guest, youâve probably got more important things to do, such as organizing your cassette tapes and LPs before shoving them in a box destined for the attic, believe me, your time will be better spent, especially when you take that stroll down memory lane and consider how killer it w</span>
  <span id="freeText4110702374742712810" style="display:none">*Ranked as one of the Top 100 Fiction of the 20th Century*<br>Iâm not quite sure how to put this in words.  Hell, Iâm not sure what I intend to say, so this is going to be ugly.  If you want to sit in on this exercise be my guest, youâve probably got more important things to do, such as organizing your cassette tapes and LPs before shoving them in a box destined for the attic, believe me, your time will be better spent, especially when you take that stroll down memory lane and consider how killer it would be to rock out to Depeche Mode and A-Ha all afternoon (itâs possible youâre one of those badasses with some Dead Kennedys on cassette, more power to you, feel free to tear off your shirt and bathe with a 40oz of Big Bear). You might want to clean your bong while youâre at it; you never know how immersed youâll get in the hazy recollections of your exploits during the New Wave or burgeoning Hardcore era and might prefer a soundtrack to complement your deranged thoughts.<br><br>	As for Lolita, maybe this tangential bullshit will help get my point acrossâ¦ Iâve recently taken a keen interest in watching âDeadliest Catchâ, but as soon as the show concludes I wonder âWhat have I walked away with, after investing 50 minutes and 4 rum &amp; cokes to the accompaniment of these awe-inspiring images of man battling the elements to strike bepincered gold?â  Not much, appears to be the answer; itâs just a bunch of crabs, crabs, crabs, and master-baiting, the simple ingredients of all my friendships and relationships.  Sure, itâs awesome to watch sea-faring maniacs risk life and limb to haul in the nasty creatures that pay their bills, but thereâs nothing else to it, just dudes getting stoked over a huge haul of crabs, or lamenting some element of the life theyâve chosen. Then you get to thinking how these dudesâ wives manage without them at sea for 20+ days at a time; obviously they are in the crab-catching business as well.  And the real kick in the ass is that I donât even eat crab, hell, Iâve never even tried a dish that incorporates any crab. When I consider the ridiculousness of it all, it seems pretty disheartening.  This sort of pointless introspection is probably how Des Esseintes got his start, so Iâll leave it at that before it becomes habit-forming.<br><br>	I canât say that my opinion of Lolita is much different.  Itâs just Humbert Humbert endlessly rhapsodizing about nymphettes, most substantially, his nymphette, Lolita.  And thatâs it; Iâve probably never read anything so one-dimensional in my life.  Iâm sure the upper echelons of literary critics found myriad reasons beyond my primitive sensibilities for including this in the Top 100 works of 20th century fiction, I just donât happen to see it.  One of these qualifiers might be this absurd statement on the back cover, âLolita is also the story of a hypercivilized European colliding with the cheerful barbarism of postwar Americaâ.  Now, when we consider the social climate at the time of publication, with book-banning and obscenity trials recently being all the rage, and you know that in order to somehow foist upon the public a tale concerning a grown manâs obsession and fornication with a 12 year old girl the defenders of this thinly-veiled smut have to somehow show that there is something above and beyond mere prurient interest hidden between the covers of the book, so statements like the one above are thrown out there to mind-f#ck all.  I donât even know what the hell that statement on the cover is supposed to mean, but âpostwar Americaâ were powerful buzzwords at the time, and truly, what else could you possibly use as a thematic defense for this book, seeing as the book has nothing to offer except the ridiculous tale of a weathered old pervert ogling young girls on the hopscotch courts, finding one that epitomizes his unacceptable desires, and eventually having his way with her before attempting to completely control her young body and mind during a trek that can only be considered kidnapping on the grandest scale.  Letâs not forget that when his precious nymphette is purloined by an unknown fiend, he goes off to ambush the guy and shoot his ass; this is not the behavior of the âhypercivilizedâ; my own rather sloppy upbringing declares that anyone with a shred of civility offers his adversary a duel rather than attack him unawares, Humbert is a lowly coward of the worst disposition. The âhypercivilizedâ donât go around bagging 85 pound kids sporting skinned knees from spills at the roller-rink, real men fish for those awesome and voluptuous Amazonians, the women who proudly stand 5â10â or more and have generous curves that modern fabrics fruitlessly struggle to contain from spilling forth in all their fully-developed glory.  Our man Humbert has no such redeeming qualities; heâs a scoundrel of the lowest and basest rank.  Letâs not forget that he weeps, and when I conjure a mental image of the elusive âhypercivilized Europeanâ, weeping like a bitch and throwing a tantrum doesnât enter the picture.<br><br>	While the frauds who compiled the Top 100 Fiction list and I might diverge in our estimations of Lolita, I will agree with our man John Updike when he states âNabokov writes prose the only way it should be written, that is, ecstatically.â  There is no denying that the book is phenomenally written, but the repetition of nymphette-this and nymphette-that simply overwhelmed me; I was left with an indelible image of Nabokov sitting there alternately referencing his thesaurus and a collection of kiddie porn for inspiration while hammering this out.  Iâd have enjoyed this book a thousand times more had it been about turtles, or walnuts, or my mother's first communion, hell, anything else.  Nymphettes suck: there is a reason I donât go trolling for trim at the nearest bus station, who the hell wants to put up with the behavior of a pubescent trollop?  <br><br>	There isnât a whole hell of a lot that I know, but I do know this; if my choices were either read Lolita again or spend a day crabbing in the arctic, Iâd better be prepared to get some frostbite on my beanbag. <br></span>

<div id="review_426889640" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/426889640" />
  <a href="/user/show/7915003-garima" class="left imgcol" title="Garima"><img alt="Garima" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/users/1360053697p2/7915003.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/426889640?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Oct 24, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7915003-garima" class="user" title="Garima">Garima</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Those who LOVE to read</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/7915003-garima?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/7915003-garima?shelf=sui-generis" class="actionLinkLite">sui-generis</a>,

                <a href="/review/list/7915003-garima?shelf=my-2-cents" class="actionLinkLite">my-2-cents</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer426889640" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15527565349536605968">The word/name Lolita always had a negative connotation for me. I became familiar with it at a relatively young age, when a famous villain in Bollywood used to say this name in a movie whenever he used to get horny on seeing a damsel (Aauu...Lalita *Lolita as I used to thought*), my Indian friends should know. And then I had an aunt I was not much fond of, whose name was Lalita and I used to call her Lolita. *unlovingly*<br><br>When I was in 6th or 7th standard, we had a Physical Education teacher (Pun f</span>
  <span id="freeText15527565349536605968" style="display:none">The word/name Lolita always had a negative connotation for me. I became familiar with it at a relatively young age, when a famous villain in Bollywood used to say this name in a movie whenever he used to get horny on seeing a damsel (Aauu...Lalita *Lolita as I used to thought*), my Indian friends should know. And then I had an aunt I was not much fond of, whose name was Lalita and I used to call her Lolita. *unlovingly*<br><br>When I was in 6th or 7th standard, we had a Physical Education teacher (Pun fully intended), who according to the complaints by some girls and the rumors that were doing the rounds was accused of inappropriate behavior towards the girl students. And the complaints were <strong>True</strong>! He was later fired (scandalously) or resigned (quietly) much to the relief of many. How we all gossiped? Oh how cheeeeeap! Doesnât he have mother or daughters at home? At that time we didnât know that there is a bloody term for such swines. The categorization of things were quite simple; it used to be either Good or Bad and in case of Bad, the reaction mostly settled at, âOh letâs not talk about itâ. Yes! Donât talk about it! The charm of convenience. In this book however, Nabokov talked and how mercilessly he did. <br><br>It was few years later that I became aware of the existence of the book named Lolita written by a writer whose name I couldnât pronounce then and already adjudged the book as DIRTY and never to be read...ever! I was not always a reader and never encouraged to be one till the age of 19 and even when I became one, I had the task of becoming a broad-minded reader from a narrow-minded one and I am solely responsible for this transition. Big Deal? No! not really, itâs just that this arrangement made me appreciate various forms of arts especially movies, music and books and then of course, Lolita became as a must-read, while I was exploring the sea of words around the world. <br><br>The brilliance of Nabokov would have been established in my mind had I read any other book by him, but Lolita, the seductress that she is, demanded to be read before any of those books and reading it is what I did. <br><br>This chef-d'oeuvre is glowing with perfection. Every word of this text is emitting a light of such splendor that for it being readable by someone, it had to dim a little by its despicable theme, <strong>Pedophilia</strong>. And that theme could be the sole reason why it is detest by many. Is it a fair reaction? Of course it is! Is the filth containing between words too much for one to handle? May be. Did Nabokov played a master stroke by pulling off one hilarious passage after another and making the reader laugh Hard while talking about Humbert's vicious intentions? Thatâs why heâs great, Right! These are some questions I asked myself on the surface, but there were many other questions waiting to be answered that had a more generic role with respect to this book. <br><br>Wikipedia describes this book as a <strong>Tragicomedy</strong> but I beg to differ. I didnât feel any remorse or sympathy towards Humbert at the end <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[when he realized his love for Dolores <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and how he was manipulated but it all seemed âfair enoughâ to me. As far as Dolores is concerned, her character sketch, her playfulness, her awareness about her impact on Humbert and the fate she faced at the end was the perfect recipe to be consumed by readers to produce a pitiful belch at the most, because as must have noticed by many, the whole damn narration is from Humbertâs point of view and poor(?) Dolores was more or less left at the mercy of worthy/unworthy readers. But since Humbert himself confessed his intentions, his dilemmas, his happiness, his sadness, he might have believed himself as a victim of a tragedy and him being an anti-hero. <br><br>But whatever is the case, it was not easy being one of the members of the so-called Jury that Humbert addressed his readers as.  There was a constant tug of war between various emotions that I experienced while reading the book. Following are some examples:<br><br><em>âI adore her so horribly.<br>No: âhorriblyâ is the wrong word. The elation with which the vision of new delights filled me was not horrible but pathetic. I qualify it as pathetic. Pathetic because despite the insatiable fire of my venereal appetite, I intended, with the most fervent force and foresight, to protect the purity of that twelve-year-old child.â</em> (Hmmmm)<br><br><em>Thrusting my fatherly fingers deep into Loâs hair from behind, and then gently clasping them around the nape of her neck, I would lead my reluctant pet to our small home for a quick connection before dinner.</em> ($%@#&amp;!)<br><br><em>The most miserable of family lives was better than the parody of incest, in which they were involved.</em> (Now youâre saying, but itâs too late.)<br><br>And the loving adjectives Humbert used for his misfortunate wife: Phocine, my brand-new large-as-life wife, etc. (Hahaha!)<br><br>I canât say what was more unbearable, the irony of the whole thing, the magnificence of Nabokovâs prose or the study of oneâs own character that could be one possible purpose behind writing this work of art. I really don't care about this interpretaion <em>Lolita is also the story of a hypercivilized European colliding with the cheerful barbarism of postwar America.</em> I was laughing while spitting expletives; I identified a potential murderer in me (Yes! If Humbert was real, I would have killed him), I questioned myself about the set of morals that govern our civilized world and consequently questioned my role in executing such morals in real life. What was good in my upbringing that Iâve become the person that Iâm today and what was/is wrong in the upbringing of Humberts and Lolitas of the world that made them self-destructive? And lastly, what is the extent of dependence of one person on another that cultivates the deadly emotions like obsession, possessiveness and LOVE. Iâm having a strong inclination towards studying Psychology.<br><br>Anyway, I recommend this book to those who LOVE to read, not like but love, else it wonât be easy to marvel at the wonder of this outstanding painting of words. <br><br>If Nabokov said that of all his books Lolita had left him with the most pleasurable afterglow, then it will left you with that afterglow too after reading it.<br><br>If Nabokov said that Lolita is his favorite and she was like the composition of a beautiful puzzle, then you would love to solve that puzzle.<br><br>If Nabokov said that he shall never regret Lolita, then being a reader one wonât ever regret reading it. </["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
