<div id="review_41825911" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41825911" />
  <a href="/user/show/1199525-cecily" class="left imgcol" title="Cecily"><img alt="Cecily" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/users/1343028343p2/1199525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/41825911?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Aug 15, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1199525-cecily" class="user" title="Cecily">Cecily</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_3" title="liked it">3 of 5 stars</a>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=miscellaneous-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">miscellaneous-fiction</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=american-canadian" class="actionLinkLite">american-canadian</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41825911" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15244841794579858338">Impossible to rate as it's an awful subject, but very well written. The skill of the book (and what makes it most disturbing for me), is that it isn't a clear-cut story of innocent child and predatory adult (which is not to excuse Humbert's actions), and it's only told from one - very biased - point of view. <br><br>Since writing this review, I've discovered that Nabokov was a <strong>synaesthete</strong>. If I (re)read him, I'll have to bear that in mind. See: <a href="http://blogs.plos.org/neurotribes/2012/02/06/inside-the-mind-of-a-synaesthete/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">http://blogs.plos.org/neurotribes/201...</a> .<br><br>This book raises</span>
  <span id="freeText15244841794579858338" style="display:none">Impossible to rate as it's an awful subject, but very well written. The skill of the book (and what makes it most disturbing for me), is that it isn't a clear-cut story of innocent child and predatory adult (which is not to excuse Humbert's actions), and it's only told from one - very biased - point of view. <br><br>Since writing this review, I've discovered that Nabokov was a <strong>synaesthete</strong>. If I (re)read him, I'll have to bear that in mind. See: <a href="http://blogs.plos.org/neurotribes/2012/02/06/inside-the-mind-of-a-synaesthete/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">http://blogs.plos.org/neurotribes/201...</a> .<br><br>This book raises many intriguing and troubling questions, balanced out by beautiful writing. Some see it as a love story, but I see no love - not even self love. The subject is appalling, but itâs not explicit, which has the disturbing effect of making the reader complicit in Humbertâs fantasies and later, his actions.<br><br>* Some minor spoilers below (but the story is well-known) *<br><br>It is written as the supposedly honest confession of a paedophile, with overtones of necrophilia (initially he intends to drug her to rape her) and incest (when he is Lolitaâs (step)father and even fantasises about her bearing him a child to replace her in his sexual affections). It mixes psychological self-analysis, wry humour, literary flourishes and endless excuses and justifications, though at other times he relishes his debauchery. Mostly he writes in a detached way, especially early on, occasionally slipping into the third person for himself. <br><br>Although itâs meant to be âtrueâ, I couldnât see why Lolita stuck with Humbert once the initial excitement had worn off, even allowing for the fact she enjoyed manipulating him for gifts and money (her loose morals, he said) and there was no one obvious for her to turn to. Nor was there any explanation as to why she was so sexually precocious (it seems to predate her fling at summer camp), which is odd, as it could have provided further justification for Humbert. Also, the bit where the headmistress of Beardsley School tells Humbert that she is âmorbidly uninterested in sexual mattersâ was bizarrely implausible.<br><br>Humbert gives plenty reasons why he should not feel guilty (ânymphetsâ are demoniac; itâs a natural urge; other societies allow such relationships; if she was drugged sheâd never know; itâs not as bad as murder; heâs generous and indulgent), but Nabokov muddies it further by the fact that Lolita is not a virgin and actually takes the initiative in their first sexual encounter and at least one subsequent one, even though she doesnât appear to enjoy it. That's enough justification for Humbert (hopefully not Nabakov), and leaves the reader unsettled and unconvinced.<br><br>The second half of the book is more muddled and, at times manic, reflecting Humbertâs own decline. After all the dreadful things he does, his final downfall is a literal but relatively trivial crossing of the line: a gloriously ironic way to end such a troubling novel.<br><br>Would such a book be published today, now society is more paranoid about paedophiles? Zoe Heller wrote Notes on a Scandal (called <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13258.What_Was_She_Thinking___Notes_on_a_Scandal__A_Novel" title="What Was She Thinking?  Notes on a Scandal  A Novel by ZoÃ« Heller">What Was She Thinking?  Notes on a Scandal  A Novel</a> in the US), but that was an older woman with a teenage boy. What does the writing of such a book say about Nabokov and, more troublingly, what does the reading of it say about me?<br><br>(My review of Notes on a Scandal is here: <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/43469719" target="_blank">http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a>.)</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9143528121270414649">Dear Mr. Nobokov,<br><br>Kindly accept my apologies for not accomplishing the mission of studying your work in the most literary sense (as I assume perhaps may be your purpose). Do consider that I had not had the slightest idea of the subject of the work beforehand, so I did not start reading it with any prejudice in my mind. No, I did not. <br><br>The beginning of the book was quite effortless. The words flowed exactly as you had designed them to flow, with the desired rhythm of course. You were quite good whe</span>
  <span id="freeText9143528121270414649" style="display:none">Dear Mr. Nobokov,<br><br>Kindly accept my apologies for not accomplishing the mission of studying your work in the most literary sense (as I assume perhaps may be your purpose). Do consider that I had not had the slightest idea of the subject of the work beforehand, so I did not start reading it with any prejudice in my mind. No, I did not. <br><br>The beginning of the book was quite effortless. The words flowed exactly as you had designed them to flow, with the desired rhythm of course. You were quite good when it came to illuminating the situation of poor Humbert. Ah, his desires, his love, Annabel, nymphets, sin, loinâ¦â¦.! Poor Humbert, on the look out for a <strong>âdemon childâ</strong>!!â¦â¦While you so elegantly explained the passionate state H.H faced every time he came across a nymphet, my mind raced to the somehow (deliberately?) missed terror in the eyes of the later, if she, by any chance, caught the poor Humbertâs intent gaze directed at her. Oh that weird feeling of understanding that mysterious look while just beginning to acknowledge the unwarranted changes, which differentiated her from her best pal sometimes, was so shocking that she wondered if any thing was wrong with her.<br><br>Believe me; I did try to understand the agony that Humbert went through when he had strenuous exercise of love making three times with the orphan (for the very first time). But I couldnât help imagining the prospective young female, out of that nymphet, who either ended up being a schizophrenic or turned out to be a wretched woman, someone who couldnât ever trust any one again! Someone, who missed out the pleasure of being a normal person, someone for whom the Sun did not shine the same way or the Rain did not ever signify the beginning of something new. <br><br>And I would have very much proceeded with the understanding, hadnât I come across the secret desire of Humbert to practice the art of being a Granddad on Lolita, the third.  <br><br>Ah there, can you notice the welling tears in my eyes? I give up. <br><br>So, this was the end of my journey through your work (which I vainly tried to achieve in nearly seven attempts). <br><br><strong>RIP Humbert</strong>. May you are never born again!<br><br>P.S. I donât think I can ever rate this book.<br><br><br></span>

<div id="review_73428826" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/73428826" />
  <a href="/user/show/1033675-matt" class="left imgcol" title="Matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/users/1325271531p2/1033675.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/73428826?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Oct 04, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1033675-matt" class="user" title="Matt">Matt</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/978170.Lolita" class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780783880808">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer73428826" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10437087642010347178">My favorite book in all of literature. When I first encountered this title at the age of nineteen I finally realized that great writers were "doing stuff" besides just telling stories. Sure, in high school there was plenty of dry discussion about symbolism and foreshadowing and even dryer in-class readings of poets like Emily Dickinson and Anne Bradstreet(Ugh!), but at that point it all sounded like "The Yellow Rose of Texas" to me. <br><br>As I cannot even begin to give this book a straight (or even ob</span>
  <span id="freeText10437087642010347178" style="display:none">My favorite book in all of literature. When I first encountered this title at the age of nineteen I finally realized that great writers were "doing stuff" besides just telling stories. Sure, in high school there was plenty of dry discussion about symbolism and foreshadowing and even dryer in-class readings of poets like Emily Dickinson and Anne Bradstreet(Ugh!), but at that point it all sounded like "The Yellow Rose of Texas" to me. <br><br>As I cannot even begin to give this book a straight (or even objective) review, I must run back to the shelter of the random thoughts format...<br><br>It seriously pisses me off that less literary-minded folk who are aware of this work always seem to refer to it as "that child molester book."<br><br>H.H. is a classic example of the unreliable narrator, but I am constantly amazed at just how inept and bumbling that character unwittingly portrays himself despite his half-truths and gold-plated recollections. Young Delores is in control of the balance of power in that relationship throughout the main narrative. His perversions seem to be of a puritanically circumspect variety as well.<br><br>H.H. suffers a mental breakdown at some point in the time-line of his confessions. Before I had always believed that it happened during the second cross-country trip, but after this reading I am now suspicious that it was in fact much earlier. <br><br>The opening two paragraphs of this work are damned near perfect in my mind...<br><br><em>Lolita, light of my life, fire of my loins. My sin, my soul. Lo-lee-ta: the tip of the tongue taking a trip of three steps down the palate to tap, at three, on the teeth. Lo. Lee. Ta.<br><br>She was Lo, plain Lo, in the morning, standing four feet ten in one sock. She was Lola in slacks. She was Dolly at school. She was Dolores on the dotted line. But in my arms she was always Lolita.</em><br><br>When you say the author's name in your head do you pronounce it NAH-bah-kov or na-BOK-ov? I ask because I recently stumbled across an interview reprint online in which he railed on the various American butcherings of his name, and then (presumably) whacked the interviewer with a butterfly net a time or two.<br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="10437087642010347178" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

This book is a snake mind pit. <br><br>It also broke my heart for the realization that knowing other people can be completely and totally foriegn. It is incredibly depressing to me how many people I've known (all female) who took Humbert's word about anything Lolita felt. Nabokov's genius was also in the telling between the lines. Those beats in spaces, the silences. How could they miss that? How could anyone get so lost in the wallowing that the</span>

Those beats in spaces, the silences. How could they miss that? How could anyone get so lost in the wallowing that they forget everything else?</span>

  <em>Lolita</em>

  <span id="freeText10818159076463060417" style="display:none">Rating: 5* of five<br><br><img src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380736362/3116991.jpg" class="escapedImg"><br><br><em>Piss Christ</em>, Andres Serrano<br><br>Like <a href="http://tinyurl.com/km5u2av" rel="nofollow">
  <em>Lolita</em>
</a>, reviewed on my blog, this work of art is one of the most challenged and banned and censored of all time. The religious are horrfied and offended that he artist submerged a representation of their tortured-to-death "savior" on his deathbed in a vat of urine.<br><br>But there's nothing in the least offensive about showing a man's death-agony. Not a bit of it.<br><br><em>Lolita</em> is challenged because it presents a pedophile's adoration of a little girl. It promotes awareness of pedophilia!<br><br>And not in a positive way, goofballs. Awareness of something is a precondition to avoiding that something.<br><br>LIKE ALL CENSORS, those calling for the censorship of this book, and of <em>Piss Christ</em>, just don't want to be bothered by things that are uncomfortable.<br><br>Too fuckin' bad. The world does not run on your itty fee-fees. Pull up your big-kid pants and walk away from things you don't like. <br><br>Us grown-ups will be out here with real world. Ignore that laughter. It can't be at you.</span>

  document.onclick = checkOpenSelects;

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('SHNQaY+BrUGNmuYa0r3VPCJoXboJY+zpES1dKDEoS68DcMbufLlIDL9xkbzJB3wYh1sivpPO3T7qe3wpmyqquA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ubzVuceEN1dH8//PXJcUjsR2o1HQUp1QSO/txYD3LE3yv0M+NLzSGnUYiGlHLb2qYUXcVUr/rIezuczEKvXNWg==')})
  }

In<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText14423672796643210117\" style=\"display:none\">WG Sebald\'s Austerlitz has something of the fractured narrative and wanderlust of his novels The Emigrants and The Rings of Saturn, and continues to develop their obsession with history, loss and memory--or more precisely in this case, forgetting. In the decade since the original German publication of Vertigo, Sebald has established himself as indisputably one of Europe\'s most interesting and lauded writers.  In 1967, the narrator bumps into a man in the salle de pas perdus of Antwerp\'s Central Station. Thus begins a long if intermittent acquaintance, during which he learns the life story of this stranger, retired architectural historian Jacques Austerlitz. Raised as Dafydd Elias by a strict Welsh Calvinist ministry family, it is only at school that Austerlitz learns his true name--and only years later, by a series of chance encounters, that he allows himself to discover the truth of his origins, as a Czech child spirited away from his mother and out of Nazi territory on the Kindertransport. He returns to confront the childhood traumas that have made him feel that &quot;I must have made a mistake, and now I am living the wrong life.&quot;  In this writer\'s hands, Austerlitz\'s tale of personal emotional repression becomes a metaphor for Europe\'s smothered past. Sebald wittily explores the tricks of time and space, unearthing Europe as an unconscious palimpsest. Delighting in lists and unfeasibly lengthy descriptions, Sebald can turn anything to poetry--even the alleged health benefits of Marienbad\'s Auschowitz springs become &quot;a positive verbal coloratura of medical and diagnostic terms&quot; (luckily, all his characters seem to be able to hold forth this way). Indeed, Sebald writes with such preternatural lucidity that even a harrowing account of writer\'s block ironically becomes a celebration of his own quite clearly unblockable virtuosity. At heart, though, Austerlitz is a serious indictment of modern Europe\'s &quot;avoidance system&quot;, its repeated patterns of personal and institutional forgetting that, even within Austerlitz\'s own lifetime, have contrived to obscure, ignore and render irretrievable his past and the source of his pain. And yet, despite the bleakness of that picture, the book ends with its hero--and its readers--committed to trying, at least, to remember. --Alan Stewart<\/span>\n  <a The story concerns Thomas Sutpen, a poor man who finds wealth and then marries into a respectable family. His ambition and extreme need for control bring about his ruin and the ruin of his family.     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/5152.Vladimir_Nabokov">About Vladimir Nabokov</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/5152.Vladimir_Nabokov"><img alt="5152" class="right" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1399321558p2/5152.jpg" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer8484126178450724164">Russian: ÐÐ»Ð°Ð´Ð¸Ð¼Ð¸Ñ ÐÐ»Ð°Ð´Ð¸Ð¼Ð¸ÑÐ¾Ð²Ð¸Ñ ÐÐ°Ð±Ð¾ÐºÐ¾Ð²<br><br>Vladimir Vladimirovich Nabokov, also known by the pen name Vladimir Sirin, was a Russian-American novelist. Nabokov wrote his first nine novels in Russian, then rose to international prominence as a master English prose stylist. He also made significant contributions to lepidoptery and had an interest in chess problems.<br><br>Nabokov's <em>Lolita</em> (1955) is frequently cit</span>
  <span id="freeText8484126178450724164" style="display:none">Russian: ÐÐ»Ð°Ð´Ð¸Ð¼Ð¸Ñ ÐÐ»Ð°Ð´Ð¸Ð¼Ð¸ÑÐ¾Ð²Ð¸Ñ ÐÐ°Ð±Ð¾ÐºÐ¾Ð²<br><br>Vladimir Vladimirovich Nabokov, also known by the pen name Vladimir Sirin, was a Russian-American novelist. Nabokov wrote his first nine novels in Russian, then rose to international prominence as a master English prose stylist. He also made significant contributions to lepidoptery and had an interest in chess problems.<br><br>Nabokov's <em>Lolita</em> (1955) is frequently cited as his most important novel, and is at any rate his most widely known one, exhibiting the love of intricate wordplay and descriptive detail that characterized all his works.<br></span>
